No, actually the opposite. 		In Shelby County v. Holder, SCOTUS gutted the preclearance provision in 2013. This means the #NCGA no longer has to get the Justice Dept. to sign off first, so it‚Äôs possible to dilute minority strength through ‚Äúcracking‚Äù (the opposite of packing). https://t.co/V6oCVBLhQJ
@EricWRowell @longleafpol Usually redistricting involves federal and state constitutional claims. There‚Äôs a case in Wake County decided a few weeks back that involved state claims.
‚ÄúThe Mecklenburg commissioners map was actually drawn by the Republican-controlled General Assembly in 2011, over Mecklenburg‚Äôs objections.‚Äù https://t.co/KixB9Ojh3Q		As I mentioned before, I support independent redistricting. At all levels. https://t.co/QI02PryxjM
@longleafpol That‚Äôs incorrect. That‚Äôs what I‚Äôm saying in my thread. Current constitutional law guards against that possibility.
So, I couldn‚Äôt disagree more with the article‚Äôs conclusion that ‚ÄúAs long as Democrats own urban areas and Republicans own rural ones, the GOP will be at an advantage in districts.‚Äù It‚Äôs not consistent with the law, history, or with a myriad of statistical studies.
This article argues that Republicans, disproportionately in the rural areas, benefit from proportional, district-based representation. The framers constructed the legislative branch in the US Constitution with the opposite argument as its premise.
One of the primary arguments for having a US Senate with equal representation by state? The more rural states feared that a proportional body would become dominated by the cities. 		Dems are now disproportionately in the cities.
Finally, the article‚Äôs premise flies in the face of why we have a proportional US House to begin with. 		During the Constitutional Convention, there was much debate about whether to have a legislature where states had equal votes or votes based on population.
(Before anyone asks, I‚Äôve said many, many times that I support independent redistricting and have voted consistent with that view in the #ncga. This is not making excuses for Dems, because gerrymandering is wrong, regardless of what party does it. I‚Äôm just reciting history.)
It‚Äôs counterintuitive, but creating a 2nd predominately-black seat actually makes partisan gerrymandering harder for Dems. 		Why?		Because districts bordering a racially-gerrymandered one actually become more Republican, not less. There are several studies demonstrating this.
Originally, Dems drew 1 predominately-black seat. At the time, plans required the Justice Dept to sign off (called ‚Äúpreclearance‚Äù). 		DOJ refused to preclear the plan, saying the #ncga needed to add another black district. That was why the 12th was added. See Shaw v. Reno.
That brings me to the next point. The article uses the old 12th Congressional as an example of why Dems have to gerrymander the state to make districts equal.		The article says ‚ÄúDemocrats originally drew that district to carve themselves out another seat.‚Äù That‚Äôs factually wrong.
While gerrymandering law can be complex, what gerrymandering means is actually pretty clear. The key aspect involves drawing lines for ‚Äúpolitical advantage.‚Äù Redistricting and gerrymandering are not synonymous. 		Also, there are various types of gerrymandering.
The article says ‚ÄúIs this gerrymandering? Not really.‚Äù The courts - both federal and state - disagree. 		Multiple courts and multiple judges - including appointees from Republican and Democratic presidents - have determined that these maps are gerrymandered, in whole or part.
Also, because our state has a ‚Äúwhole county rule‚Äù in its constitution, counties must be grouped together (also based on population) before districts are even drawn. Groupings (or ‚Äúclusters‚Äù) are based on a mathematical formula that neither party can avoid using.
Is geography a factor? Yes (for example, districts must be contiguous). However, geography (and, importantly, density and distributive demographics) does not override population reqs.
Controlling factors for districts are population and proportionality. These are rigid rules under case law. 		Urban districts have to have the same number of people in them as rural ones and vice versa.
Under federal case law, legislative maps have to be as close to equal in population as possible. For Congressional districts, the population must be as identical as possible among districts. State leg districts can have a 5% difference from the average, plus or minus.
In other words, this article would be just as flawed if it had argued that geography inevitably leads to Dems winning more seats than Republicans in NC. 		One constitutional requirement undercuts this argument entirely - ‚Äúone person, one vote.‚Äù
First, let me preface this by saying neither geography nor case law makes it inevitable that *either* party will have an advantage over the other without gerrymandering - particularly in the current environment, where our state is pretty evenly split politically.
This article claims that ‚Äúgeography‚Äù inevitably leads to Republicans winning more seats than Dems in NC. 		This is fundamentally false. 		Why? Because federal and state constitutional law says so. 		Here‚Äôs a thread explaining why. #ncpol #ncga https://t.co/kus09hIg0h

In short, our constitution makes budgets unique. It subjects them to transparency that other bills don‚Äôt necessarily have. It keeps these bills from being passed too quickly. 		In short, budgets are important - so important that they‚Äôre set apart. #ncpol #ncga

@Robyn780 Thanks! Just trying to demystify a lot of this stuff so we all know what's up
What the special master concludes could set off a chain reaction w/ major implications for 2018 #ncga elections. Stay tuned. 15/15 #ncpol
Impact of a special master depends on 1) how many districts must change &amp; 2) how districts around those districts will change too. 14/#ncpol
To summarize, fixing one illegal district can mean a bunch of districts change. Changing 7 House districts could affect many more. 13/#ncpol
Remember criteria about how population size can only be off by 5%? House 105 is off by 4.4%. Mine's off by 4.3%. Not much wiggle room. 12/
Redrawing 105 will require either adding or subtracting people. Those people come from neighboring districts, changing their size. 11/#ncpol
For example, House 105 (in light blue) may be redrawn. It touches 3 districts, including mine. 10/#ncpol https://t.co/0c1EJLD8gZ
Remember threads I did about all the criteria &amp; how they're pulling in diff directions? That makes a domino effect more likely. 9/#ncpol
Redrawing districts is squeezing a balloon - the air has to go somewhere. Can lead to domino effect of multiple districts changing. 8/#ncpol
Another impt point: you can't redraw just one district. To redraw one is to basically redraw the districts touching it. 7/#ncpol
One important point: appointing a special master does not mean any districts will be redrawn, but it does make it more likely. 6/#ncpol
Special master's job is to 1) tell the Court if it thinks the specific districts mentioned are illegal and 2) redraw them if so. 5/#ncpol
What the court decided to do was appoint a "special master" - basically an expert that helps the Court make an informed decision. 4/#ncpol

@DavidTa84534406 There's so little case law around this, it's not as straightforward as a potential leg challenge.
My prediction: #HB717 may redraw the courts but could very well end up in the courts. Watch this space. 14/14 #ncga #ncpol
In short, while both are "redistricting," leg and judicial operate under totally different rules that can lead to totally diff results. 13/
Also major reduction in number of judges elected from certain urban (read: Dem) counties, such as Durham. 12/
Lots of case law in leg redistricting involves race - doesn't apply in judicial. #HB717 could result in major decrease in black judges. 11/
If this were a leg map, such a huge variation in population would fail constitutionally. Big difference in legislative vs. judicial. 10/
Take a look at Superior Ct map - 41 districts that vary widely in population. One in Meck has 546k people. A district nearby has 88k. 9/ https://t.co/9dEBvvcZm3
So, while leg redistricting involved a lot of criteria, there isn't much law guiding this process. That brings us to today. 8/
I say "necessarily" because a future court could say legislative redistricting case law does apply to judicial. Stay tuned on that. 7/
Most federal case law on "one person, one vote" exists in the legislative redistricting context - doesn't (necessarily) apply for judges 6/
That brings us to federal law and how it affects legislative maps (vs. judicial ones). It's basically a lot of case law vs. very little. 5/
NC constitution is very specific about when leg maps drawn - only in the year after a census. No such req for judicial maps. 4/
For example, the Gov can't veto legislative maps - but can veto judicial ones. Expect these maps to require 3/5 vote to become law. 3/
First, let's start with the NC Constitution. While there's a pretty specific process for leg redistricting, there isn't for judicial. 2/
Remember all those threads I did about legislative redistricting? Judicial redistricting is totally different. Here's how. #ncga #ncpol 1/ https://t.co/RQcuXC52Th
Now, we're starting debate on judicial redistricting. HB 717 would be a complete overhaul of our judicial districts. #ncga #ncpol

I'm praying &amp; hoping for the best in #PuertoRico, but the recovery will prob be long. Let's not forget them when the storm passes. 15/15
Ive spent a lotta time in PR - I call it my home away from home. When I saw #HurricaneMaria's track it shook me bc I knew what it meant. 14/
#Irma came through the island not too long ago. Many lost power and road access that they hadn't got back before #HurricaneMaria struck. 13/
There's a political aspect too. PR is in the middle of a debt crisis. Its infrastructure has suffered from cuts that make it vulnerable. 12/
So you have an intense hurricane cutting across a mountainous island, with a lot of people living in the most intense part of the storm. 11/
Hurricanes are usually strongest on their north-northeastern side. Guess what ended up on the NNE side of #HurricaneMaria? San Juan. 10/
Here's the track of #HurricaneMaria through PR. About 3.5mil people live there, with 1mil people in or around San Juan. 9/ https://t.co/bNsGP3I7nl
In short, rain from #HurricaneMaria is rushing down from the PR mountains quickly, only to hit high sea water on rain-soaked coasts. 8/
Plus, when clouds ride up mtns, they squeeze more water out. Combine that w/ storm surge on nearby coasts. The water has nowhere to go. 7/
#HurricaneMaria is dumping huge amounts of water on PR. Estimates are 2+ ft in some places. That water has to go somewhere. 6/
So, the rain that falls in PR's central mtns goes downhill to the sea really quickly. That brings us to #HurricaneMaria specifically. 5/
In contrast, the tallest mtn east of the Mississippi is here in NC. It's 6,600 ft tall but hundreds of miles from the coast. 4/
For perspective, the tallest mtn in PR is about 4000 feet tall. It's a 20 mile drive from the city of Ponce, which is on the coast. 3/
Physically, PR is an island of stark contrasts. It goes from sea level to mountains in only a few miles. 2/
#HurricaneMaria couldn't have come at a worse time and place for Puerto Rico. Here's a thread on why. 1/ #Hurac√°nMar√≠a https://t.co/P4bzPSZHqE
